    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="57d89340636444c580547ad27a9bed58">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '99ba8f09-f651-46e3-813f-cd0a6bb6d68a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d142694386" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium (Na) metal battery is regarded as one of the most promising candidates for large-scale energy storage devices, benefiting from the abundant sodium reserves and low cost. However, its practical application is hindered by the dendrite growth and unstable electrode-electrolyte interface. Herein, a 3D sodiophilic structure composed of a carbon matrix overlaid with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> coating layers (<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C) is designed to stabilize the Na plating/stripping behavior. The sodiophilicity is endowed by a highly reversible adsorption-intercalation process at the hetero-interface, which can guide conformal Na deposition and induce the formation of inorganic-rich solid-electrolyte interphases with high structural stability and fast Na-ion transport. Meanwhile, the 3D scaffold can effectively accommodate Na deposition during Na plating/stripping and depress the dendrite formation. As a result, the half cell assembled with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode delivers long-term cycling performance at 1.0 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> with a high Coulombic efficiency of 99.92% for over 2000 cycles and of 99.94% even at 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. The practical feasibility of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C is verified with full cells, which shows favorable rate capability and long-cycle performance. The sodiophilic hetero-interface construction strategy proposed in this work sparks new insights for designing high-performance Na metal anodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403258-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the boosting demands of large-scale energy storage, the requirement for rechargeable batteries is continuously increasing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0001_R_d142694377e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0003_R_d142694377e473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium metal has a high theoretical capacity (1166 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and low redox voltage (−2.71V vs standard hydrogen electrode), leading it to be a promising anode for high-density batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0004_R_d142694377e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0005_R_d142694377e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, benefiting from the abundant reserves and low cost, sodium possesses economic advantages over lithium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0006_R_d142694377e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Lett.">6</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0007_R_d142694377e486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, due to its high chemical activity, problems such as dendrite growth, severe side reactions, anode pulverization, and formation of inactive sodium metal (known as “dead Na”) have led to poor cycling performance and potential safety hazards of sodium metal batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0008_R_d142694377e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0014_R_d142694377e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nanoscale">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In order to develop the feasibility of sodium metal battery applications, researchers have brought up numerous strategies, including constructing stable anodes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0015_R_d142694377e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0016_R_d142694377e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> building artificial solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0017_R_d142694377e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> optimizing liquid electrolyte composition,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0018_R_d142694377e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> designing advanced separator,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0019_R_d142694377e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and utilizing solid-state electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0020_R_d142694377e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3D hosts have been proven to be a valid strategy for improving the structure stability of anodes during the charge/discharge processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0021_R_d142694377e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">21</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0023_R_d142694377e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy. Environ. Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The porous 3D structure is capable of alleviating the volume change and reducing the local current density effectively, leading to a reduction of the concentration polarization and an improvement of electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0024_R_d142694377e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Energy. Mater.">24</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0027_R_d142694377e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Sci. Adv.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Carbonaceous frameworks have been widely investigated for good electrical conductivity, lightweight, and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0028_R_d142694377e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Energy. Environ. Sci.">28</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0034_R_d142694377e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a primitive sodiophobic carbon matrix induces uneven sodium nucleation and dendritic sodium deposition, resulting in unsatisfactory cycling performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0035_R_d142694377e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem.">35</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0038_R_d142694377e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Sci. Adv.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, improving the sodium affinity is crucial for achieving the practical application of carbonaceous substrates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0039_R_d142694377e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Res.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> So far, various sodiophilic nucleating agents such as metal or metal oxide nanoparticles, and oxygen/nitrogen-containing functional groups have been used to improve the surface sodiophilicity of carbon substrates.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0037_R_d142694377e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0039_R_d142694377e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Res.">39</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0051_R_d142694377e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These active sodiophilic sites usually rely on alloying reactions or chemical reactions with sodium metal to guide uniform sodium nucleation and deposition, which exhibits certain improving effects during the initial cycling process. However, the aforementioned sodiophilic nucleating agents still face the risk of pulverization and detachment upon continuous plating/stripping process, which will deteriorate the cycling stability of sodium metal batteries. Therefore, rather than introducing additional sodiophilic agents, promoting the intrinsic sodiophilicity of the substrate, can be an advantageous strategy to guarantee uniform Na deposition upon long-term cycling.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, a novel adsorption-intercalation mechanism was proposed to guide conformal sodium deposition within a sodiophilic interface, which is highly reversible and stable during the plating/stripping process. Specifically, a 3D carbon scaffold (3D-C) covered with insulating <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (denoted as <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C) was fabricated and utilized as a porous host for sodium metal, in which the sodiophilic <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layers with abundant Na-ion conducting channels guarantee homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> flux and enable the uniform Na plating. In this design, sodium ions first adsorb onto N-atom hexagonal ring centers of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer, and then intercalate into the hetero-interface between <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and 3D-C, achieving a unique surface adsorption-intercalation process to guide conformal Na deposition and suppress Na dendrite growth. Benefiting from the avoidance of the alloying reactions, the risk of active sodium metal pulverization and detachment is significantly suppressed. Besides, the 3D structure effectively reduces the local current density and alleviates the volume changes during Na plating/stripping, further inhibiting the Na dendrite formation. As a result, half cells assembled with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C delivered a high average Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.92% for over 2000 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Long-term cycling stability was achieved with a high CE of 99.94% under a strict testing condition of 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Full cells using <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) exhibit good high-rate compatibility and stable cycling performance for over 650 cycles with a capacity retention of up to 87.57%.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403258-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthesis process of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C was schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1a</a> (see Experimental Section for more details). Specifically, the 3D-C substrate was prepared via freeze-drying and calcination, during which a homogeneous and porous structure was obtained with the aid of the salt templates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1b</a>). Afterward, the dicyandiamide was uniformly coated on 3D-C to obtain the dicyandiamide/3D-C precursor (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The precursor was then calcinated to form a consistent <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C hetero-interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1c</a>). The porous structure endows the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C composite with a high pore volume of 0.35 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> and a large specific surface area of 218.71 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), which can produce abundant active sites to reduce the local current density and accommodate Na deposition during Na plating/stripping effectively. Fourier transform infrared spectrometer (FT-IR) was employed to identify the constitution of the prepared composite. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1d</a>, different from the bare 3D-C, characteristic peaks of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> located at 810 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 1200–1650 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be observed in the spectrum of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C, indicating the existence of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0052_R_d142694377e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns showed, the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C peak center was located at 25.38° as the dashed line marked, which could be fitted to two major peaks corresponding to Carbon (light blue) and <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (light yellow) respectively, illustrating the introduction of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0053_R_d142694377e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Angew. Chem.">53</a>-<a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0057_R_d142694377e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403258-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/400a1c02-4559-4c71-94b3-3fba158c8f9b/aenm202403258-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/400a1c02-4559-4c71-94b3-3fba158c8f9b/aenm202403258-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b34f8d6f-bf14-4bd6-a255-5f66cf8474ff/aenm202403258-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/400a1c02-4559-4c71-94b3-3fba158c8f9b/aenm202403258-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403258-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403258" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the fabrication process of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C and characterizations of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C. a) Schematic illustration of the fabrication process of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C. b,c) SEM images of 3D-C and <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C. d) FT-IR spectra of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (red), 3D-C (blue), and <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (yellow). e) TEM image and the corresponding elemental mapping of C (red) and N (green) in <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C composite. f) Elemental compositions acquired by XPS measurement on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C. Inset in (f): high-resolution N 1s spectrum of 3D-C (black) and <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (red).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HR-TEM) images of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C presented that the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layers were uniformly coated on the surface of 3D-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the homogenous distributions of C and N were confirmed by the elemental mapping images of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1e</a>). The element composition and bonding state of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C were revealed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). Characteristic peaks of C, N, and O elements can be observed in the survey spectrum of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), with the N element content accounting for 5.85% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1f</a>). High-resolution N 1s XPS spectrum of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C composite (inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0001">1f</a>) contains C<span class="icomoon"></span>NC peak (398.8eV), N-(C)<sub>3</sub> peak (400.1eV) and N-H peak (401.2eV), confirming the existence of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0058_R_d142694377e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Langmuir">58</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0059_R_d142694377e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Adv. Mater.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the mass loading of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> in <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C is measured to be 10.65% using the thermogravimetric analysis-differential scanning calorimetry (TG-DSC, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>In order to evaluate the effect of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> on enhancing sodiophilicity, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed. The charge density difference diagrams based on DFT calculations were simulated, presenting the electron rearrangement in the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C interface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Electrons deplete the N-atom hexagonal ring centers and accumulate under the triazine units, making the former centers negative for Na ion adsorption. The density of states (DOS) revealed that the electron transfer rate of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C was raised (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7c</a>, Supporting Information). The <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer brought in the reconstruction of electron distribution, facilitating the electronic conduction and the Na ion adsorption. A structure model of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C was established based on the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and graphene crystal structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). As reported by DFT simulations, Na ions are preferentially adsorbed on the available N-atom hexagonal ring centers (n = 1 − 4, ΔE<sub>nNa</sub> &lt; 0, <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a–d</a>, Supporting Information). As the number of deposited Na ions increases, N-atom hexagonal ring centers are gradually filled in, and the following Na ions (n ≥ 5) intercalate at the van der Waals gap between <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and 3D-C rather than deposits on the surface of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (ΔE<sub>5Na-interface</sub> &lt; 0, ΔE<sub>5Na-surface</sub> &gt; 0, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9e–i</a>, Supporting Information). Consistent with the DFT calculation results, an obvious slope in the capacity-voltage curve of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode, can be attributed to the ion adsorption on N-atom hexagonal ring centers and intercalation at the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C interface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2b</a>). This adsorption-intercalation mechanism promotes the sodiophilicity of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C components, benefiting the homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> flux and uniform Na deposition. In contrast, the adsorption energy of 3D-C for the Na ion is above zero (n = 1–4, ΔE<sub>nNa</sub>&gt;0, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), illustrating that the 3D-C structure is sodiophobic. As for <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer, the Na ions are preferentially adsorbed on the N-atom hexagonal ring centers of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (n = 1–4, ΔE<sub>nNa</sub>&lt;0, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11c–f</a>, Supporting Information), and when the fifth Na ion is introduced, the forming energy becomes positive (n = 5, ΔE<sub>5Na</sub> = 0.288 eV&gt;0, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11g</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that surficial Na plating is thermodynamically unfavorable. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information, the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C presents a nucleation overpotential of 17 mV at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, which is much lower than that of the bare 3D-C (37 mV) and bare Al (100 mV). Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) analyses were conducted to reveal the Na metal deposition behaviors on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C, 3D-C, and bare Al electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Before the Na deposition, it was revealed that the porous structure of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C composite was well maintained after being coated on Al foil (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2c1</a>). At low deposition areal capacities, the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode showed a dendrite-free and smooth surface, benefiting from the uniform sodiophilic surface and stable 3D nanostructure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2c2</a>). When the areal capacity was increased to 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode still presented a flat surface without Na dendrites, acting as a stable host to effectively induce the smooth Na deposition and alleviate the large volume change (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2c3</a>). Subsequently, after total stripping, the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C structure remains intact and uniform, showing high plating/stripping reversibility (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2c4</a>). At a fixed areal deposition capacity of 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, a great mass of Na metal with a loose structure appeared on the surface of the 3D-C electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c</a>, Supporting Information), in sharp contrast to the dense Na deposition and smooth surface of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2c3</a>), verifying the important role of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer in guiding the uniform Na plating behavior. As for bare Al foil, irregular Na agglomerations were displayed after Na metal deposition (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). In the cross-section images, the island-like Na metal was highlighted by dashed circles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14c</a>, Supporting Information), and large size lumpy protrusions after 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> indicated the inhomogeneity of Na metal deposition on bare Al electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14f</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403258-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7d6711ff-1485-438d-a0c9-36cb26dc73cc/aenm202403258-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7d6711ff-1485-438d-a0c9-36cb26dc73cc/aenm202403258-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6319f801-238e-42f3-b294-88fd59a047d7/aenm202403258-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7d6711ff-1485-438d-a0c9-36cb26dc73cc/aenm202403258-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403258-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403258" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Theoretical explanation and Na plating/stripping behaviors on the electrodes of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C and Al. a) DFT simulation of Na plating on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/C heterostructures. b) Capacity-voltage curves of Na plating on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (red), 3D-C (blue), and Al (black) at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Voltage profiles of Na plating/stripping on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, and c<sub>1</sub>–c<sub>4</sub>) corresponding top-view SEM images of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrodes at various discharging/charging states. d,e) In situ optical observation of Na deposition on (d) Al and (e) <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C at a current density of 10 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. The scale bars are 50 µm. f) In situ XRD pattern of Na plating/stripping on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In situ optical microscope experiments were further conducted to intuitionally observe the Na deposition process at the current density of 10 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> on different electrodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2d</a> e; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). After Na metal deposition for 5 min, the surface of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode was still smooth, while Na dendrites were visible on the surface of bare Al foil. As the plating time increased up to 10 min, the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode maintained a dendrite-free surface, and in contrast, the growth of dendrites on the bare Al surface was detected. The 3D-C framework was able to accommodate Na metal for 5 min, while small dendrites appeared in 10 min, showing limited ability for Na deposition (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). The aptitude for hampering Na metal dendrite growth and accommodating sodium metal strongly ensures the stability of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode, which is one of the key factors for reversible and stable Na plating/stripping performance. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0002">2f</a> displays the in situ XRD variation of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode during the discharging/charging process. The Na metal characteristic peak of (110) planes (PDF No. 22–0948) appeared during the middle range of the discharging process, and the peak intensity decreased when the procedure was shifted to charging. At the end of charging, the signal of Na metal completely disappeared, indicating the complete stripping of Na metal and high plating/stripping reversibility on the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode. An in situ XRD pattern of 3D-C and bare Al electrode was provided in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). On the 3D-C electrode, at the end of charging, the signal of the Na metal characteristic peak still existed with weak intensity, suggesting tiny Na aggregation left in the 3D-C electrode. When bare Al foil was used as an anode, the intensity of the Na metal characteristic peak arose immediately upon discharging, and remained detectable at the end of charging, indicating the residual Na clusters. These phenomena signify that the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode with a sodiophilic surface guides the uniform Na deposition and enables the reversible Na stripping/plating process.</p>
         
         <p>The cycling performance of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode was tested with galvanostatic charge/discharge processes. The charge-discharge curve within 100 cycles at a current density of 1mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and an areal deposition capacity of 1mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> is displayed in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0003">3a</a>. The well-overlapped curves at the 1st, 50th, and 100th cycles prove that the nucleation and deposition overpotentials during the plating/stripping process are quite stable. In addition, the voltage curve of the 3D-C electrode shows erratic slop in the charging procedure, and the voltage polarization curves of bare Al foil fluctuated (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the morphologies of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C, bare Al foil, and 3D-C electrode after 50 cycles of unceasing plating/stripping process are detected using SEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19–S21</a>, Supporting Information). The <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode maintained its original porous structure, and the corresponding elemental mapping images showed the uniform distribution of Na element, which was attributed to the homogeneous SEI, illustrating no “dead Na” formation after 50 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). In comparison, the 3D-C skeleton was broken, and the thick interface between the 3D-C surface and the electrolyte suffered from fluctuations, indicating the unstable environment in the cells with 3D-C electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Island-like spots can be observed in the digital image of the bare Al electrode after 50 cycles, which corresponds to the “dead Na” with a loose structure in SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0003">3b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). The excellent capability of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode to restrain the “dead Na” formation makes it possible to keep the electrode stable during the charge/discharge process, leading to a reliable anode host for sodium metal batteries.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403258-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f97f497f-616c-48b4-9075-1331a1643d85/aenm202403258-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f97f497f-616c-48b4-9075-1331a1643d85/aenm202403258-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/417d098d-a738-41f0-b432-ffec875da5d2/aenm202403258-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f97f497f-616c-48b4-9075-1331a1643d85/aenm202403258-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403258-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403258" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Morphologies and compositions of electrodes after Na plating/stripping processes. a) Voltage profile of charging/discharging process in half-cell of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C for 100 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Inset in a): Voltage profiles of charging/discharging process in half-cell of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C at the 1st, 50th, and 100th cycle. b) SEM images of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C and bare Al electrode before and after 50 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. Inset in b): optical photographs of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C and bare Al electrode before and after 50 cycles at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Elemental compositions of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C and bare Al electrodes after different sputtering times. d) XPS spectra of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode after different sputtering times.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The SEI components are important for suppressing side reactions and stabilizing electrodes during long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0008_R_d142694377e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0026_R_d142694377e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to detect SEI structure and analyze the interfacial information, XPS depth profiling combined with the ion beam etching method was conducted. The proportions of O on the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode were lower than those on bare Al foil (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0003">3c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, a comparison among XPS spectra of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode at different sputtering times is displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a</a> (Supporting Information), intuitively demonstrating the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C SEI composition evolution. The F 1s spectra revealed the existence of NaF (683.7 eV) and P-F (687.7 eV) species, and the Na 1s spectrum was fitted to Na-O/Na-F at 1171.0 eV, suggesting a NaF-rich SEI in the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0060_R_d142694377e1478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - ACS Cent. Sci.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C 1s peak was fitted by 3 peaks, involving C<span class="icomoon"></span>C/C<span class="icomoon"></span>H (284.8 eV), C<span class="icomoon"></span>O (286.3 eV), and CO (289.0 eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0061_R_d142694377e1483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Mater. Chem. A">61</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0062_R_d142694377e1483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Small">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensities of CO and C<span class="icomoon"></span>O peaks were relatively weak, and the O<span class="icomoon"></span>C (533.4eV) and OC (531.6 eV) peaks attenuated while the Na<sub>2</sub>O peak (530.4 eV) increased in O spectra, implying few organic compounds in the internal SEI. In summary, a SEI structure agreed with an organic (external)-inorganic (internal) heterogeneous model was formed in <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode, which is highly impermeable to the electrolyte solvent, preventing the side reactions between electrolyte and Na metal, and having good ionic conductivity to facilitate uniform Na plating underneath.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0063_R_d142694377e1497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Nano. Energy.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS spectra of the bare Al electrode at different sputtering times are exhibited (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), and the strong peaks of O and C illustrated that the SEI of the bare Al electrode was organic compound-rich. The favorable NaF-rich SEI of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode prevents continuous SEI formation/breaking and plays an important role in promoting uniform Na deposition with evenly distributed Na<sup>+</sup> flux.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0027_R_d142694377e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Sci. Adv.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0042_R_d142694377e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0050_R_d142694377e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Half-cells were assembled and tested for long-term cycling to further evaluate the performance of g-C3N4/3D-C in practical conditions. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0004">4a</a>, a high average CE of 99.92% was achieved for more than 2000 cycles within the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C cell at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, demonstrating the highly stable and reversible Na plating/stripping process. In comparison, the CEs of cells with bare Al and 3D-C electrodes fluctuated severely. At 3 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>, cells with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode can be stably cycled for up to 1100 cycles with an average CE over 99.9%, while CEs of cells using bare Al and 3D-C started undulating from the beginning (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, cells with the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode maintained a stable average CE of 99.94% for more than 260 cycles when the current density and deposition capacity were elevated to 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0004">4b</a>). Such stable CEs under extreme testing conditions benefited from the sodiophilic surface and the 3D structure of the electrode, consistent with the aforementioned results. The cycling performance of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C half cells and the corresponding testing parameters are compared with other previous reports in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0004">4c</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0027_R_d142694377e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Sci. Adv.">27</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0037_R_d142694377e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0038_R_d142694377e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Sci. Adv.">38</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0055_R_d142694377e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Nano">55</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0064_R_d142694377e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Energy. Storage. Mater.">64</a>, <a href="#aenm202403258-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202403258-bib-0065_R_d142694377e1583" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Adv. Mater.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is demonstrated that <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C has superior stability and compatibility with demanding cycling conditions. The voltage hysteresis curves of the cells using <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C, 3D-C, and bare Al electrodes on cycling were displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information). For the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode, the polarization between the charge and discharge plateaus was small and stable, and the curves of the charging/discharging procedure were highly symmetrical, proving the superior reversibility of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode. In comparison, the charging curves of the 3D-C electrode showed drastic oscillations, and the voltage curves of the Al electrode demonstrated high overpotentials and fluctuated charging capacities. Then, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were utilized at different cycles to characterize the interfacial stability and electrochemical kinetics with different electrodes. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0004">4d</a>, <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C had a quite low and stable resistance after 200 cycles compared with 3D-C. Detailed fitting results (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrated that the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode displayed steady SEI resistance (R<sub>SEI</sub>) and charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) with cycling, while the 3D-C electrode showed increasing R<sub>SEI</sub> as the cycle number grew. The incremental R<sub>SEI</sub> of the 3D-C electrode was attributed to the thickened SEI layer caused by the uncontrollable side reactions between the electrolyte and the Na metal during the constant plating/stripping process, proving the importance of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> in promoting interfacial sodiophilicity and stability. Cells with bare Al electrode exhibited large resistance compared with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information), which hindered the electrochemical process seriously.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202403258-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82d82cbc-ab0b-4720-bd08-b8217759d568/aenm202403258-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/82d82cbc-ab0b-4720-bd08-b8217759d568/aenm202403258-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b1e86dc8-8a42-4a93-abee-40f4a97dcf40/aenm202403258-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82d82cbc-ab0b-4720-bd08-b8217759d568/aenm202403258-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202403258-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202403258" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cycling stability of Na plating/stripping and electrochemical performances of full cells based on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrodes. a) CE of Na deposition on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (red), 3D-C (blue), and Al (black) with a capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at 1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) CE of Na deposition on <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (red), 3D-C (blue), and Al (black) with a capacity of 10 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> at 5 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Comparison of cycle stability, current density, and areal capacity with published works. d) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy measurements of batteries with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (red) and 3D-C (blue) after different cycles. e) Long-term cycling performances of Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||NVP (red), Na-3D-C||NVP (blue), and Na-Al||NVP (black) at 3C. f) Rate and cycling performances of Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||NVP (red), Na-3D-C||NVP (blue), and Na-Al||NVP (black).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Symmetric cells with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C, 3D-C, bare Al electrodes plated with 3 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup> Na metal were assembled to explore the electrode stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). For Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C symmetric cells, stable cycle life for over 600 h was achieved with low overpotential ranging from 10 to 13 mV. In contrast, Na-3D-C||Na-3D-C was burdened with large voltage polarization, and the Na-Al||Na-Al symmetric cell suffered from voltage fluctuation and failed after 450h. Full cells were assembled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> electrodes as the cathode to further assess the practical feasibility of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0004">4e</a>, the Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||NVP cell delivered an initial specific discharge capacity of 84.04 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 89.89% after 250 cycles at a charge rate of 3C, while the CEs of Na-3D-C||NVP cell and Na-Al||NVP cell declined rapidly after 100 and 20 cycles, respectively. The rate capacities of the full cells were measured at the current densities ranging from 0.05C to 5C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202403258-fig-0004">4f</a> ). When tested at a high rate of 5C, the Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||NVP cell maintained 85.42% of the capacity at 0.5C, while the Na-Al||NVP cell only maintained 61.64% of its capacity at 0.5C. Furthermore, as the charge rate was set back to 1C, the Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||NVP cell could deliver a capacity of 90.00 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high capacity retention of 87.57% after 650 cycles. Corresponding voltage profiles of the Na-<i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C||NVP cells showed that the voltage hysteresis increased slowly (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the excellent electrochemical kinetics of full cells. In contrast, Na-3D-C||NVP cell only worked for 100 cycles at 1C. Full cells with a limited N/P ratio were tested to illustrate the cycling performances under harsh working conditions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). The <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode maintained a stable electrochemical performance for 120 cycles with a capacity retention of 92.82%. In comparison, the specific capacity of the full cells with Na-3D-C and Na-Al electrodes deteriorated quickly in 70 and 30 cycles, respectively. These performances illustrate that the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C electrode has a magnificent performance of long-term cycles in the full cells, which provides the possibility of practical application in sodium metal batteries. The strong Na affinity of the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C allowed the 3D porous structure to maximize the capability to accommodate Na deposition and restrain dendrite growth, contributing to higher capacity retentions and better cell stabilities.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403258-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, a 3D sodiophilic structure consisting of a porous carbon matrix coated with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> was proposed for sodium metal batteries. The sodiophilic surface for uniform sodium metal deposition was formed by a highly reversible adsorption-intercalation process at the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C hetero-interface. The designed framework provides a guarantee for homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> flux and inorganic-rich SEI, stabilizing the long-cycle performance of sodium metal batteries. A high CE of 99.92% for over 2000 cycles was realized, and full cells matched with NVP showed excellent cycle stability. These electrochemical tests declared the practical potential of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C as a sodium metal anode. The strategy to control the behavior of Na deposition by introducing <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> surface on 3D-C represents the feasibility of the practical application of next-generation sodium metal batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202403258-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202403258-sec-0070-title"> Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>NaCl, glucose, dicyandiamide, and NMP were purchased from Aladdin. Na metal, Al foil, NVP powder, super P powder, and PVDF powder were supplied by MTI Corporation. Electrolytes (NP-005, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme and NC-008, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DEC (1:1 by volume) with 5wt.% FEC) were purchased from Suzhou Duo Duo Co., Ltd. Polypropylene membrane was purchased from Celgard Co., Ltd.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202403258-sec-0080-title"> Preparation of the 3D-C and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C</h4>
                  
                  <p>First of all, 3D-C was prepared through the salt templet method using NaCl and glucose as precursors. 40 g of NaCl and 4 g of glucose were added to 200 mL of deionized water, and the liquid was stirred for 2 h to dissolve the reagents completely. The uniform solution was frozen and freeze-dried to obtain the homogeneous composite of NaCl-glucose. The composite was put in a tube furnace (Thermo Scientific) and treated by two-stage calcination under an Ar atmosphere. The temperature was elevated at the rate of 10&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> and was held at 760&nbsp;°C for 1 h, then heated and kept at 1000&nbsp;°C for 1 h. After this procedure of calcination, the obtained compound was washed to get rid of recrystallized NaCl and acquire pure 3D-C. Later on, 400 mg 3D-C and 400 mg dicyandiamide were put into 200mL deionized water, followed by sonicating for 1 h and stirring for another 2 h. The suspension liquid was frozen and freeze-dried to procure dicyandiamide/3D-C composite. This composite was heated at the ramping rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> and then calcinated in a tube furnace at 550&nbsp;°C for 2 h under Ar protection.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202403258-sec-0090-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD was performed on a Rigaku SmartLab SE diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation. SEM images were captured on a Hitachi SU8020 instrument. TEM images were obtained using JEOL JEM-F200 microscopy. FT-IR was performed on a Thermo Fisher Scientific Nicolet iS20 spectrometer. XPS measurements were conducted on Thermo Scientific K-Alpha equipped with Ar<sup>+</sup> ion etching. TG-DSC spectra were obtained on PerkinElmer STA 8000.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202403258-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electrodes were prepared through the typical slurry-making process. The mass ratio of <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (or 3D-C) and polyvinylidene difluoride were 4:1, which were mixed and stirred in an appropriate amount of N-methyl pyrrolidone (NVP) for 4 h to obtain a uniform slurry. The slurry was coated on bare Al foil by doctor blading and then exsiccated by vacuum drying at 80&nbsp;°C for at least 12 h to get rid of NVP completely.</p>
                  
                  <p>All cells were assembled in a glove box filled with Ar, and tested on a Neware system. The charge/discharge procedures were carried out in CR2025 coin-type half cells, with <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/3D-C (3D-C or bare Al foil) electrode as the working electrode and Na foil as the reference and counter electrode. 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme was added in half cells as an electrolyte to investigate the sodium plating/stripping process, while Celgard 2325 was used to separate the current collector and Na foil.</p>
                  
                  <p>EIS measurements were conducted using a CHI760E electrochemical workstation (Shanghai Chenhua), with a frequency ranging from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz and an amplitude voltage of 5 mV.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202403258-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202403258-sec-0110-title"> Theoretical Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed employing the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP). A structure model was established based on the <i>g</i>-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and graphene crystal structure. Energies of deposited Na were approximately calculated using formation energy (ΔE<sub>f</sub>), and it was expressed as ΔE<sub>f</sub> = E<sub>total</sub>(n)-E<sub>total</sub>(n-1)-E(Na), where E<sub>total</sub>(n) and E<sub>total</sub>(n-1) denote the total substrate energy with n Na and (n-1) Na, respectively.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
